<p><b>Ralph Nader</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ər/ 'er' in 'finger'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span>; born February 27, 1934)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> is an American political activist, author, lecturer, and attorney, noted for his involvement in <a href="/wiki/Consumer_protection" title="Consumer protection">consumer protection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a>, and government reform causes. The son of Lebanese immigrants to the United States, Nader was educated at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard">Harvard</a> and first came to prominence in 1965 with the publication of the bestselling book <i><a href="/wiki/Unsafe_at_Any_Speed" title="Unsafe at Any Speed">Unsafe at Any Speed</a></i>, a critique of the safety record of American automobile manufacturers that became known as one of the most important journalistic pieces of the 20th century. Following the publication of <i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i>, Nader led a group of volunteer law students - dubbed "Nader's Raiders" - in a groundbreaking investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a>, leading directly to that agency's overhaul and reform. In the 1970s, Nader leveraged his growing popularity to establish a number of advocacy and watchdog groups including the <a href="/wiki/Public_Interest_Research_Group" title="Public Interest Research Group">Public Interest Research Group</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Auto_Safety" title="Center for Auto Safety">Center for Auto Safety</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Public_Citizen" title="Public Citizen">Public Citizen</a>.</p>
<p>Nader's activism has been directly credited with the passage of several landmark pieces of American consumer protection legislation including the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Product_Safety_Act" title="Consumer Product Safety Act">Consumer Product Safety Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Corrupt_Practices_Act" title="Foreign Corrupt Practices Act">Foreign Corrupt Practices Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower_Protection_Act" title="Whistleblower Protection Act">Whistleblower Protection Act</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Traffic_and_Motor_Vehicle_Safety_Act" title="National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act">National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act</a>, and he has been repeatedly named to lists of the "100 Most Influential Americans", including those published by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_Magazine" title="Life Magazine">Life Magazine</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" title="Time Magazine">Time Magazine</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>, among others. He has run for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> on several occasions as an independent and <a href="/wiki/Third_party_(United_States)" title="Third party (United States)">third party</a> candidate, using the campaigns to highlight under reported issues and a perceived need for electoral reform.</p>
<p>A two-time <a href="/wiki/Nieman_Fellowship" title="Nieman Fellowship">Nieman Fellow</a>, Nader is the author or co-author of more than two-dozen books, and was the subject of a documentary film on his life and work, <i><a href="/wiki/An_Unreasonable_Man" title="An Unreasonable Man">An Unreasonable Man</a></i>, which debuted at the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a>.</p>
<p></p>

</li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<p>Ralph Nader was born in <a href="/wiki/Winsted,_Connecticut" title="Winsted, Connecticut">Winsted, Connecticut</a>, to Nathra and <a href="/wiki/Rose_Nader" title="Rose Nader">Rose (née Bouziane) Nader</a>, both of whom were immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washington_Times_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Times-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battling_for_Democracy_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Battling_for_Democracy-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader-Childhood_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nader-Childhood-6">[6]</a></sup> After settling in Connecticut, Nathra Nader worked in a textile mill before opening a bakery and restaurant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unreasonableman_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-unreasonableman-7">[7]</a></sup> Ralph Nader occasionally helped at his father's restaurant, as well as worked as a newspaper delivery boy for the local paper, the Winsted <i>Register Citizen</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nation11_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-nation11-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nader graduated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Gilbert_School" title="The Gilbert School">The Gilbert School</a> in in 1951, going on to attend <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>. Though offered a scholarship to Princeton, Nader's father forced him to decline the offer on the grounds that the family was able to pay Nader's tuition and the funds should go to a student who could not afford it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Nader graduated <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magna_cum_laude" title="Magna cum laude">magna cum laude</a></i> with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson_School_of_Public_and_International_Affairs" title="Woodrow Wilson School of Public and International Affairs">Woodrow Wilson School of Public and International Affairs</a> in 1955.</p>
<p>After graduating from Princeton, Nader began studying at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a>, though quickly became bored by his courses. While at Harvard, Nader would frequently skip classes to hitchhike across the U.S. where he would engage in field research on <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> issues and migrant worker rights. He ultimately earned a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LL.B." title="LL.B.">LL.B.</a> from Harvard in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nation11_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-nation11-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>After graduating from Harvard, Nader served in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a> as a cook and was posted to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Dix" title="Fort Dix">Fort Dix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nation11_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-nation11-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<p>In 1959 Nader was admitted to the bar and began practice as a lawyer in <a href="/wiki/Hartford,_Connecticut" title="Hartford, Connecticut">Hartford, Connecticut</a>, while also lecturing at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hartford" title="University of Hartford">University of Hartford</a> and traveling to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, where he filed dispatches for the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Science_Monitor" title="Christian Science Monitor">Christian Science Monitor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nation" title="The Nation">The Nation</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nation11_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-nation11-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1964, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, taking a position as a consultant to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_Labor" title="Secretary of Labor">Assistant Secretary of Labor</a> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Patrick_Moynihan" title="Daniel Patrick Moynihan">Daniel Patrick Moynihan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unsafe_at_Any_Speed"><i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i></span></h3>
<p>Nader was first propelled into the national spotlight with the 1965 publication of his journalistic expose <i><a href="/wiki/Unsafe_at_Any_Speed" title="Unsafe at Any Speed">Unsafe at Any Speed</a></i>. Though he had previously expressed an interest in issues of automobile safety while a law student, <i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i> presented a critical dissection of the automotive industry by claiming that many American automobiles were generally unsafe to operate. Nader researched case files from more than 100 lawsuits then pending against <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>' <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvair" title="Chevrolet Corvair">Chevrolet Corvair</a> to support his assertions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:141px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unsafeatanyspeedcover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="221" data-file-width="139" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/ca/Unsafeatanyspeedcover.jpg" width="139"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unsafeatanyspeedcover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i> propelled Nader to national attention.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The book became an immediate bestseller but also prompted a vicious backlash from General Motors (GM) who <a href="/wiki/Smear_campaign" title="Smear campaign">attempted to discredit Nader</a> by tapping his phone in an attempt to uncover salacious information and, when that failed, hiring prostitutes in an attempt to catch him in a compromising situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Nader, by then working as an unpaid consultant to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abe_Ribicoff" title="Abe Ribicoff">Abe Ribicoff</a>, reported to the senator that he suspected he was being followed. Ribicoff convened an inquiry that called GM CEO James Roche who admitted, when placed under oath, that the company had hired a private detective agency to investigate Nader. Nader sued GM for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_privacy" title="Invasion of privacy">invasion of privacy</a>, settling the case for $425,000 and using the proceeds to found the activist organization the Center for the Study of Responsive Law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nation11_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-nation11-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>A year following the publication of <i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i>, Congress unanimously enacted the <a href="/wiki/National_Traffic_and_Motor_Vehicle_Safety_Act" title="National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act">National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act</a>. <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the United States House of Representatives</a> <a href="/wiki/John_William_McCormack" title="John William McCormack">John William McCormack</a> said the passage of the National Traffic and Motor Vehicle Safety Act was due to the “crusading spirit of one individual who believed he could do something: Ralph Nader".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Nader.27s_Raiders.22_and_Public_Citizen">"Nader's Raiders" and Public Citizen</span></h3>
<p>In 1968 Nader recruited seven volunteer law students, dubbed "Nader's Raiders" by the Washington press corps, to evaluate the efficacy and operation of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> (FTC). The group's ensuing report, which criticized the body as "ineffective" and "passive" led to an <a href="/wiki/American_Bar_Association" title="American Bar Association">American Bar Association</a> investigation of the FTC. Based on the results of that second study, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> revitalized the agency and sent it on a path of vigorous consumer protection and antitrust enforcement for the rest of the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the publication of the report, in 1971, Nader founded the watchdog group <a href="/wiki/Public_Citizen" title="Public Citizen">Public Citizen</a> to engage in public interest lobbying and activism on issues of consumer rights, serving on its board of directors until 1980.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s.E2.80.931990s">1970s–1990s</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NADER_AND_FORD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="779" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/NADER_AND_FORD.jpg/300px-NADER_AND_FORD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/NADER_AND_FORD.jpg/450px-NADER_AND_FORD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/NADER_AND_FORD.jpg/600px-NADER_AND_FORD.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NADER_AND_FORD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nader, far right, at a meeting with <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Porter" title="Sylvia Porter">Sylvia Porter</a>, and U.S. president <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> in 1974.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surging_popularity">Surging popularity</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg"><img alt="Young-looking Nader at 40+ years old gesturing as he speaks, wearing a coat and tie with unruly wavy dark hair." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3976" data-file-width="2657" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg/170px-Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg/255px-Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg/340px-Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ralph-Nader-1975.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nader in 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the early 1970s Nader had established himself as a household name. In a critical memo written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_F._Powell,_Jr." title="Lewis F. Powell, Jr.">Lewis Powell</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Chamber_of_Commerce" title="U.S. Chamber of Commerce">U.S. Chamber of Commerce</a>, Powell warned business representatives that Nader "has become a legend in his own time and an idol of millions of Americans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ralph Nader's name appeared in the press as a potential candidate for president for the first time in 1971, when he was offered the opportunity to run as the presidential candidate for the New Party, a progressive split-off from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>. Chief among his advocates was author <a href="/wiki/Gore_Vidal" title="Gore Vidal">Gore Vidal</a>, who touted a 1972 Nader presidential campaign in a front-page article in <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire</a></i> magazine in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Best_Man.2F.2772_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Best_Man.2F.2772-17">[17]</a></sup> Nader declined the advances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justin_Martin_2002_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Justin_Martin_2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1974 he received the S. Roger Horchow Award for Greatest Public Service by a Private Citizen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Continued_activism">Continued activism</span></h4>
<p>In the 1970s, Nader turned his attention to environmental activism, becoming a key leader in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antinuclear_movement" title="Antinuclear movement">antinuclear power movement</a>, described by one observer as the "titular head of opposition to nuclear energy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_Mass_(Anti-nuclear_group)" title="Critical Mass (Anti-nuclear group)">Critical Mass Energy Project</a> was formed by Nader in 1974 as a national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> umbrella group, growing to become the largest national <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement_in_the_United_States" title="Anti-nuclear movement in the United States">anti-nuclear group in the United States</a>, with several hundred local affiliates and an estimated 200,000 supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The organization's main efforts were directed at lobbying activities and providing local groups with scientific and other resources to campaign against nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wr_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-wr-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Ralph_Nader_lectures_at_Florida_State_University_1980s.ogv/640px--Ralph_Nader_lectures_at_Florida_State_University_1980s.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:428px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="82.918458049887" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Ralph_Nader_lectures_at_Florida_State_University_1980s.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Ralph_Nader_lectures_at_Florida_State_University_1980s.ogv" type="video/ogg; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ralph_Nader_lectures_at_Florida_State_University_1980s.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ralph Nader lectures at Florida State University, 1980s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout the 1970s and 1980s, through his ongoing work with Public Citizen, Nader continued to be involved in issues of consumer rights and public accountability. His work testifying before Congress, drafting model legislation, and organizing citizen letter-writing and protest efforts, earned him direct credit for the enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Corrupt_Practices_Act" title="Foreign Corrupt Practices Act">Foreign Corrupt Practices Act</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Product_Safety_Act" title="Consumer Product Safety Act">Consumer Product Safety Act</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower_Protection_Act" title="Whistleblower Protection Act">Whistleblower Protection Act</a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_campaigns">Presidential campaigns</span></h3>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_campaign_history">Presidential campaign history</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1972">1972</span></h4>
<p>Ralph Nader's name appeared in the press as a potential candidate for president for the first time in 1971, when he was offered the opportunity to run as the presidential candidate for the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=New_Party_(United_States,_1972)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="New Party (United States, 1972) (page does not exist)">New Party</a>, a progressive split-off from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> in 1972. Chief among his advocates was author <a href="/wiki/Gore_Vidal" title="Gore Vidal">Gore Vidal</a>, who touted a 1972 Nader presidential campaign in a front-page article in <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire</a></i> magazine in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Best_Man.2F.2772_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Best_Man.2F.2772-17">[17]</a></sup> Psychologist <a href="/wiki/Rockway_Institute" title="Rockway Institute">Alan Rockway</a> organized a "draft Ralph Nader for President" campaign in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> on the New Party's behalf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Nader declined their offer to run that year; the New Party ultimately joined with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(United_States,_1970s)" title="People's Party (United States, 1970s)">People's Party</a> in running <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Spock" title="Benjamin Spock">Benjamin Spock</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1972" title="United States presidential election, 1972">1972 presidential election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justin_Martin_2002_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Justin_Martin_2002-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Spock had hoped Nader in particular would run, getting "some of the loudest applause of the evening" when mentioning him at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Spock went on to try to recruit Nader for the party among over 100 others, and indicated he would be "delighted" to be replaced by any of them even after he accepted the nomination himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Nader received one vote for the vice-presidential nomination at the <a href="/wiki/1972_Democratic_National_Convention#Delegate_vote_for_vice-presidential_nomination" title="1972 Democratic National Convention">1972 Democratic National Convention</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1992">1992</span></h4>
<p>Nader stood in as a <a href="/wiki/Write-in_candidate" title="Write-in candidate">write-in</a> for "none of the above" in both the 1992 <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_primary" title="New Hampshire primary">New Hampshire Democratic and Republican Primaries</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and received 3,054 of the 170,333 Democratic votes and 3,258 of the 177,970 Republican votes cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> He was also a candidate in the 1992 Massachusetts Democratic Primary, where he appeared at the top of the ballot (in some areas, he appeared on the ballot as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_(politician)" title="Independent (politician)">independent</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1996">1996</span></h4>
<p>Nader was drafted as a candidate for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Party_(United_States)" title="Green Party (United States)">Green Party</a> ticket during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1996" title="U.S. presidential election, 1996">1996 presidential election</a>. He was not formally nominated by the <a href="/wiki/Greens/Green_Party_USA" title="Greens/Green Party USA">Green Party USA</a>, which was, at the time, the largest national Green group; instead he was nominated independently by various state Green parties (in some states, he appeared on the ballot as an independent). However, many activists in the Green Party USA worked actively to campaign for Nader that year. Nader qualified for ballot status in 22 states,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> garnering 685,297 votes or 0.71% of the popular vote (fourth place overall),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> although the effort did make significant organizational gains for the party. He refused to raise or spend more than $5,000 on his campaign, presumably to avoid meeting the threshold for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Elections_Commission" title="Federal Elections Commission">Federal Elections Commission</a> reporting requirements; the unofficial Draft Nader committee could (and did) spend more than that, but the committee was legally prevented from coordinating in any way with Nader himself.</p>
<p>Nader received some criticism from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_rights" title="Gay rights">gay rights</a> supporters for calling gay rights "gonad politics" and stating that he was not interested in dealing with such matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> However, more recently, Nader has come out in support of <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>His 1996 running mates included: Anne Goeke (nine states), Deborah Howes (Oregon), Muriel Tillinghast (New York), Krista Paradise (Colorado), Madelyn Hoffman (New Jersey), Bill Boteler (Washington, D.C.), and <a href="/wiki/Winona_LaDuke" title="Winona LaDuke">Winona LaDuke</a> (California and Texas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2000">2000</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader_presidential_campaign,_2000" title="Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2000">Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2000</a></div>
<p>In the 2006 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/An_Unreasonable_Man" title="An Unreasonable Man">An Unreasonable Man</a></i>, Nader describes how he was unable to get the views of his public interest groups heard in Washington, even by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinton_Administration" title="Clinton Administration">Clinton Administration</a>. Nader cites this as one of the primary reasons that he decided to actively run in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_2000" title="U.S. presidential election, 2000">2000</a> election as candidate of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">Green Party</a>, which had been formed in the wake of his 1996 campaign.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="757" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG/220px-DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG/330px-DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG/440px-DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DebateCommissionProtest_2000.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nader's supporters, with <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> speaking, protest his exclusion from the televised debates in 2000</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In June 2000, The Association of State Green Parties (ASGP) organized the national nominating convention that took place in Denver, Colorado, at which Green party delegates nominated Ralph Nader and Winona LaDuke to be their party's candidates for president and vice president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 9, the <a href="/wiki/Vermont_Progressive_Party" title="Vermont Progressive Party">Vermont Progressive Party</a> nominated Nader, giving him ballot access in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> On August 12, the <a href="/wiki/United_Citizens_Party" title="United Citizens Party">United Citizens Party</a> of South Carolina chose Ralph Nader as its presidential nominee, giving him a ballot line in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_Citizens_Party_Picks_Nader_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-United_Citizens_Party_Picks_Nader-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2000, at the largest Super Rally of his campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> held in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a>, 15,000 people paid $20 each<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> to hear Nader speak. Nader's campaign rejected both parties as institutions dominated by corporate interests, stating that <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> were "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tweedledee_and_Tweedledum" title="Tweedledee and Tweedledum">Tweedledee and Tweedledum</a>". A long list of notable celebrities spoke and performed at the event including <a href="/wiki/Susan_Sarandon" title="Susan Sarandon">Susan Sarandon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ani_DiFranco" title="Ani DiFranco">Ani DiFranco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Harper" title="Ben Harper">Ben Harper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Robbins" title="Tim Robbins">Tim Robbins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Vedder" title="Eddie Vedder">Eddie Vedder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patti_Smith" title="Patti Smith">Patti Smith</a>. The campaign also had some prominent union help: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_Nurses_Association" title="California Nurses Association">California Nurses Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Electrical,_Radio_and_Machine_Workers_of_America" title="United Electrical, Radio and Machine Workers of America">United Electrical Workers</a> endorsed his candidacy and campaigned for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000, Nader and his running mate Winona LaDuke received 2,883,105 votes, for 2.74 percent of the popular vote (third place overall),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> missing the 5 percent needed to qualify the Green Party for federally distributed public funding in the next election, yet qualifying the Greens for ballot status in many states.</p>
<p>Nader often openly expressed his hope for Bush's victory over Gore, saying it "would mobilize us"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and that environmental and consumer regulatory agencies would fare better under Bush than Gore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meet_the_Press_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meet_the_Press-46">[46]</a></sup> When asked which of the two he'd vote for if forced, Nader answered "Bush… If you want the parties to diverge from one another, have Bush win."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> As to whether he would feel regret if he caused Gore's defeat, Nader replied "I would not — not at all. I’d rather have a provocateur than an anesthetizer in the White House."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> On another occasion, Nader answered this question with: "No, not at all… There may be a cold shower for four years that would help the Democratic Party… It doesn’t matter who is in the White House."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meet_the_Press_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meet_the_Press-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_spoiler_controversy">The spoiler controversy</span></h5>
<p>A common claim is that Nader's candidacy acted as a <a href="/wiki/Spoiler_effect" title="Spoiler effect">spoiler</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2000" title="United States presidential election, 2000">2000 U.S. presidential election</a>, in which 537 votes gave <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida,_2000" title="United States presidential election in Florida, 2000">a crucial and controversial victory in Florida</a> (Nader received almost 100,000 votes in Florida, from which a slight decrease in favour of Gore would have altered the outcome). Others, including Nader, dispute this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burden_673.E2.80.93699_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burden_673.E2.80.93699-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Florida,_2000" title="United States presidential election in Florida, 2000">2000 presidential election in Florida</a>, George W. Bush defeated Al Gore by 537 votes. Nader received 97,421 votes, which led to claims that he was responsible for Gore's defeat. Nader, both in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Crashing_the_Party" title="Crashing the Party">Crashing the Party</a></i> and on his website, states: "In the year 2000, exit polls reported that 25% of my voters would have voted for Bush, 38% would have voted for Gore and the rest would not have voted at all."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a> at first argued that Florida was so close that votes for any of seven other candidates could also have switched the results,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Moore_message_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Moore_message-53">[53]</a></sup> but in 2004 joined the view that Nader had helped make Bush president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-query.nytimes.com_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-query.nytimes.com-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> When asked about claims of being a spoiler, Nader typically points to the <a href="/wiki/Bush_v._Gore" title="Bush v. Gore">controversial Supreme Court ruling</a> that halted a Florida recount, Gore's loss in his home state of Tennessee, and the "quarter million Democrats who voted for Bush in Florida."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study in 2002 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Progressive_Review" title="Progressive Review">Progressive Review</a></i>, found no correlation in pre-election polling numbers for Nader when compared to those for Gore. In other words, most of the changes in pre-election polling reflect movement between Bush and Gore rather than Gore and Nader, and they conclude from this that Nader was not responsible for Gore's loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>An analysis conducted by Harvard Professor B.C. Burden in 2005 showed Nader did "play a pivotal role in determining who would become president following the 2000 election", but that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Contrary to Democrats’ complaints, Nader was not intentionally trying to throw the election. A spoiler strategy would have caused him to focus disproportionately on the most competitive states and markets with the hopes of being a key player in the outcome. There is no evidence that his appearances responded to closeness. He did, apparently, pursue voter support, however, in a quest to receive 5% of the popular vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burden_673.E2.80.93699_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burden_673.E2.80.93699-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Chait" title="Jonathan Chait">Jonathan Chait</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Prospect" title="The American Prospect">The American Prospect</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> notes that Nader did indeed focus on swing states disproportionately during the waning days of the campaign, and by doing so jeopardized his own chances of achieving the 5% of the vote he was aiming for.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Then there was the debate within the Nader campaign over where to travel in the waning days of the campaign. Some Nader advisers urged him to spend his time in uncontested states such as New York and California. These states – where liberals and leftists could entertain the thought of voting Nader without fear of aiding Bush – offered the richest harvest of potential votes. But, Martin writes, Nader – who emerges from this account as the house radical of his own campaign – insisted on spending the final days of the campaign on a whirlwind tour of battleground states such as Pennsylvania and Florida. In other words, he chose to go where the votes were scarcest, jeopardizing his own chances of winning 5 percent of the vote, which he needed to gain federal funds in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prospect1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-prospect1-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>When Nader, in a letter to environmentalists, attacked Gore for "his role as broker of environmental voters for corporate cash," and "the prototype for the bankable, Green corporate politician," and what he called a string of broken promises to the environmental movement, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Club" title="Sierra Club">Sierra Club</a> president <a href="/wiki/Carl_Pope_(environmentalist)" title="Carl Pope (environmentalist)">Carl Pope</a> sent an open letter to Nader, dated 27 October 2000, defending Al Gore's environmental record and calling Nader's strategy "irresponsible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> He wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>You have also broken your word to your followers who signed the petitions that got you on the ballot in many states. You pledged you would not campaign as a spoiler and would avoid the swing states. Your recent campaign rhetoric and campaign schedule make it clear that you have broken this pledge... Please accept that I, and the overwhelming majority of the environmental movement in this country, genuinely believe that your strategy is flawed, dangerous and reckless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2004_presidential_campaign">2004 presidential campaign</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader_presidential_campaign,_2004" title="Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2004">Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2004</a></div>
<p>Nader announced on December 24, 2003, that he would not seek the Green Party's nomination for president in 2004, but did not rule out running as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_(politician)" title="Independent (politician)">independent candidate</a>.</p>
<p>Ralph Nader and Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> held a widely publicized meeting early in the 2004 presidential campaign. Nader said that John Kerry wanted to work to win Nader's support and the support of Nader's voters, prompting Nader to provide Kerry more than 20 pages of issues that he felt were important. According to Nader, he asked John Kerry to choose any three of the issues and highlight them in his campaign; should Kerry meet these conditions Nader would not contest the election. On February 22, 2004, having not heard back from Kerry, Nader announced that he would run for president as an independent.</p>
<p>Due to concerns about a possible <a href="/wiki/Spoiler_effect" title="Spoiler effect">spoiler effect</a>, many Democrats urged Nader to abandon his 2004 candidacy. <a href="/wiki/Terry_McAuliffe" title="Terry McAuliffe">Terry McAuliffe</a> stated that Nader had a "distinguished career, fighting for working families", and that McAuliffe "would hate to see part of his legacy being that he got us eight years of George Bush". Theresa Amato, Nader's national campaign manager in 2000 and 2004, later alleged that McAuliffe offered to pay-off Nader if he would not campaign in certain states, an allegation confirmed by Nader and undisputed by McAuliffe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nader received 463,655 votes, for 0.38 percent of the popular vote, placing him in third place overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_presidential_campaign">2008 presidential campaign</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader_presidential_campaign,_2008" title="Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2008">Ralph Nader presidential campaign, 2008</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1,_October_4,_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2470" data-file-width="3086" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg/220px-Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg/330px-Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg/440px-Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1%2C_October_4%2C_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ralph_Nader_in_Waterbury_1,_October_4,_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nader campaigning in October 2008</div>

<p>In February 2007, Nader criticized Democratic front-runner <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> as "a panderer and a flatterer", later describing her as someone who had "no political fortitude".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> During a February 2008 appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Press" title="Meet the Press">Meet the Press</a></i>, Nader announced his intention to run for president as an independent, later naming <a href="/wiki/Matt_Gonzalez" title="Matt Gonzalez">Matt Gonzalez</a> as his running-mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Nader was endorsed by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Zinn" title="Howard Zinn">Howard Zinn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Ventura" title="Jesse Ventura">Jesse Ventura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Jeffre" title="Justin Jeffre">Justin Jeffre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Morello" title="Tom Morello">Tom Morello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Val_Kilmer" title="Val Kilmer">Val Kilmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Anderson" title="Rocky Anderson">Rocky Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Abourezk" title="James Abourezk">James Abourezk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patti_Smith" title="Patti Smith">Patti Smith</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jello_Biafra" title="Jello Biafra">Jello Biafra</a>. The Nader campaign raised $8.4 million in campaign funds, primarily from small, individual donations. Nader/Gonzalez earned 738,475 votes and a third-place finish in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">United States presidential election, 2008</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEC_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FEC-66">[66]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_activities">Later activities</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="D.C._Library_Renaissance_Project">D.C. Library Renaissance Project</span></h4>
<p>In 2002, Nader founded the D.C. Library Renaissance Project, which has sought to halt the development of the West End Library in Washington, D.C., alleging that it "violated affordable housing guidelines, undervalued the land, and didn't conform to the city's Comprehensive Plan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The legal obstacles presented by the Library Renaissance Project have cost the D.C. government over one million dollars in legal fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washingtoncitypaper.com_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washingtoncitypaper.com-69">[69]</a></sup> Nader has opposed the privatized development of D.C. libraries despite community support, citing a lack of oversight and competitive bidding process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Only_the_Super_Rich_Can_Save_Us"><i>Only the Super Rich Can Save Us</i></span></h4>
<p>In 2009 Nader published his first work of fiction, <i>Only the Super Rich Can Save Us</i>. Many of the characters were fictionalized versions of real-life persons including <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a>. The book's principal villain, a "conservative evil genius" named Brovar Dortwist, represents <a href="/wiki/Grover_Norquist" title="Grover Norquist">Grover Norquist</a>. According to Norquist, Nader had called him prior to the book's publication and said he "wouldn’t be too unhappy, because the character was principled”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The novel met with mixed reviews with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i> noting that the book "reads less like a novel ... than a dream journal" with a plot that victoriously concludes with "American society thoroughly Naderized", though the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globe_and_Mail" title="Globe and Mail">Globe and Mail</a></i> called it "a powerful idea by the perfect person at a fortuitous time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_debate_moderator">2012 debate moderator</span></h4>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012" title="United States presidential election, 2012">United States presidential election, 2012</a>, Nader moderated a debate for third party candidates at Washington D.C.'s <a href="/wiki/Busboys_and_Poets" title="Busboys and Poets">Busboys and Poets</a>, what <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> described as a "hipster coffeehouse/bookstore". The debate was attended by Green Party presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Jill_Stein" title="Jill Stein">Jill Stein</a>, Libertarian <a href="/wiki/Gary_Johnson" title="Gary Johnson">Gary Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Anderson" title="Rocky Anderson">Rocky Anderson</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Justice_Party_(United_States)" title="Justice Party (United States)">Justice Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitution Party (United States)">Constitution Party</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Virgil_Goode" title="Virgil Goode">Virgil Goode</a>. He later moderated a similar debate in a studio appearance broadcast by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russia_Today" title="Russia Today">Russia Today</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Museum_of_Tort_Law">American Museum of Tort Law</span></h4>
<p>In 2015, after a decade planning, Nader founded the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Tort_Law" title="American Museum of Tort Law">American Museum of Tort Law</a> in <a href="/wiki/Winsted,_Connecticut" title="Winsted, Connecticut">Winsted, Connecticut</a>. The opening ceremonies were emceed by <a href="/wiki/Phil_Donahue" title="Phil Donahue">Phil Donahue</a>. Nader personally donated $150,000 to the establishment of the museum, which was sited on two parcels of land rezoned by the town of Winsted to host it. At the time of its opening, some expressed skepticism that a museum dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tort</a> would have much interest to the general public, though Nader responded that he was "astounded how a country can go over 200 years and not have a law museum".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_Harvard_admissions_reform">Campaign for Harvard admissions reform</span></h4>
<p>Nader unsuccessfully sought a seat on the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> Board of Overseers in 2016 as part of an insurgent candidate slate operating under the name "Free Harvard, Fair Harvard" which called for increased transparency by the university as to how it made athletic and legacy admissions decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In February of that year he expressed support for <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> making a third-party run for president, saying that such a move might help break-up the two party system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>Nader was raised in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battling_for_Democracy_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Battling_for_Democracy-3">[3]</a></sup> His siblings are <a href="/wiki/Laura_Nader" title="Laura Nader">Laura</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professor</a> of social and cultural <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">U.C. Berkeley</a>), <a href="/wiki/Claire_Nader" title="Claire Nader">Claire</a>, and brother <a href="/wiki/Shafeek_Nader" title="Shafeek Nader">Shafeek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nader-Childhood_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nader-Childhood-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>He has lived in Washington, DC since the 1960s, but is domiciled in <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, where he is registered to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washingtoncitypaper.com_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Washingtoncitypaper.com-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to English, Nader also speaks <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality_and_character_traits">Personality and character traits</span></h3>
<p>Nader has been described as an "ascetic ... bordering on self-righteous".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Despite access to respectable financial assets, he famously lives in a modest apartment and spends $25,000 on personal bills, conducting most of his writing on a <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> According to popular accounts of his personal life, he does not own a <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, relies primarily on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transportation" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a>, and over a 25-year period, until 1983, exclusively wore one of a dozen pairs of shoes he had purchased at a clearance sale in 1959. His suits, which he reports he purchases at sales and outlet stores, have been the repeated subject of public scrutiny, being variously described as "wrinkled", "rumpled", and "styleless". A newspaper story once described Nader as a "conscientious objector to fashion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nader has never married. Karen Croft, a writer who worked for Nader in the late 1970s at the Center for Study of Responsive Law, once asked him if he had ever considered getting married, to which he reportedly responded that he had made a choice to dedicate his life to career rather than family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span></h3>
<p>According to the mandatory fiscal disclosure report that he filed with the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission" title="Federal Election Commission">Federal Election Commission</a> in 2000, Nader owned more than $3 million worth of stocks and mutual fund shares; his single largest holding was more than $1 million worth of stock in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems,_Inc." title="Cisco Systems, Inc.">Cisco Systems, Inc.</a> He also held between $100,000 and $250,000 worth of shares in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magellan_Fund" title="Magellan Fund">Magellan Fund</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Nader said he owned no car and owned no real estate directly in 2000, and said that he lived on $25,000 a year, giving most of his stock earnings to many of the over four dozen non-profit organizations he had founded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_appearances">Media appearances</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span></h3>
<p>In the 2005 <a href="/wiki/Jim_Carrey" title="Jim Carrey">Jim Carrey</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Fun_with_Dick_and_Jane_(2005_film)" title="Fun with Dick and Jane (2005 film)">Fun with Dick and Jane</a></i>, Nader makes a cameo appearance as himself.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Steve_Skrovan" title="Steve Skrovan">Steve Skrovan</a> documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/An_Unreasonable_Man" title="An Unreasonable Man">An Unreasonable Man</a></i> is about the life of Ralph Nader and uses both archival footage and original interviews. It debuted at the <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a> in 2006.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periodicals">Periodicals</span></h3>
<p>Nader has been featured on the cover of the January 22, 1968 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i>; the December 12, 1969 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">TIME</a></i>; the June 1971 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire</a></i>; and the August 2016 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Standard" title="Pacific Standard">Pacific Standard</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span></h3>
<p>Nader has been a guest on multiple episodes of <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Real_Time_with_Bill_Maher" title="Real Time with Bill Maher">Real Time with Bill Maher</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Show" title="The Daily Show">The Daily Show</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_O%27Reilly_Factor" title="The O'Reilly Factor">The O'Reilly Factor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Press" title="Meet the Press">Meet the Press</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_Now!" title="Democracy Now!">Democracy Now!</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Late_Show_with_David_Letterman" title="The Late Show with David Letterman">The Late Show with David Letterman</a></i>. In 2003 he appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Da_Ali_G_Show" title="Da Ali G Show">Da Ali G Show</a></i> and, in 2008, was interviewed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triumph_the_Insult_Comic_Dog" title="Triumph the Insult Comic Dog">Triumph the Insult Comic Dog</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/Late_Night_with_Conan_O%27Brien" title="Late Night with Conan O'Brien">Late Night with Conan O'Brien</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SesameStreetNader.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="400" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b2/SesameStreetNader.png/220px-SesameStreetNader.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b2/SesameStreetNader.png/330px-SesameStreetNader.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b2/SesameStreetNader.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SesameStreetNader.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 1988 Nader appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1988, Nader made an historic appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i> as "a person in your neighborhood", the episode also featuring <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Walters" title="Barbara Walters">Barbara Walters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martina_Navratilova" title="Martina Navratilova">Martina Navratilova</a>. Nader's appearance on the show was memorable because it was the only time that the grammar of the last line of the song –  "a person who you meet each day" –  was questioned and changed. Nader refused to sing a line which he deemed grammatically improper, so a compromise was reached by which Nader sang the last line solo, with the modified words: "a person <i>whom</i> you meet each day."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibliography_of_Ralph_Nader" title="Bibliography of Ralph Nader">Bibliography of Ralph Nader</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span></h2>
<ul>
<li>In 1967 Nader was named one of the "Ten Outstanding Young Men of the Year" by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Junior_Chamber" title="United States Junior Chamber">United States Junior Chamber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1990 Nader was listed one of the "100 Most Influential Americans of the 20th century by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_Magazine" title="Life Magazine">Life Magazine</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1999 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" title="Time Magazine">Time Magazine</a></i> named Nader one of the "100 Most Influential Americans of the 20th Century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Peace_Award_Medallion_-_front_side.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Gandhi_Peace_Award_Medallion_-_front_side.jpg/220px-Gandhi_Peace_Award_Medallion_-_front_side.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Gandhi_Peace_Award_Medallion_-_front_side.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Peace_Award_Medallion_-_front_side.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nader was the 2016 recipient of the Gandhi Peace Award.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>In 1999 a <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> panel of journalists ranked <i>Unsafe at Any Speed</i> 38th among the top 100 pieces of journalism of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2006 Nader was named one of the "100 Most Influential Americans" by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2008 Nader was listed among the "100 Most Influential Americans" in the <i>Britannica Guide to 100 Most Influential Americans</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2016 Nader was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_Hall_of_Fame" title="Automotive Hall of Fame">Automotive Hall of Fame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2016 Nader received that year's <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Peace_Award" title="Gandhi Peace Award">Gandhi Peace Award</a> from <a href="/wiki/Promoting_Enduring_Peace" title="Promoting Enduring Peace">Promoting Enduring Peace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<ul>
<li>The song "Fast Cars" from the <a href="/wiki/Buzzcocks" title="Buzzcocks">Buzzcocks</a>' 1978 album <i><a href="/wiki/Another_Music_in_a_Different_Kitchen" title="Another Music in a Different Kitchen">Another Music in a Different Kitchen</a></i> includes the line "sooner or later, you're gonna listen to Ralph Nader",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> referring to his efforts to raise awareness of lackluster vehicular safety standards.</li>
<li>Ralph Nader is mentioned in season 2, episode 23, "Semi-Friendly Persuasion" of the TV series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_A-Team" title="The A-Team">The A-Team</a></i>, aired in 1983. In the episode, Templeton Peck impersonates a government agent from the Bureau of Weights and Measures who is investigating a crooked hardware store owner. During his conversation with the store owner, Peck begins, "We love guys like you. Ralph eats them for breakfast." The store owner replies, "Ralph?" to which Peck responds, "Nader! You're his kinda guy! Crooked, and proud of it!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>The October 14, 2000 episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a></i> features an animated portrayal of Nader in a <i><a href="/wiki/TV_Funhouse" title="TV Funhouse">TV Funhouse</a></i> episode. In the episode, Nader has teamed-up with <a href="/wiki/Pat_Buchanan" title="Pat Buchanan">Pat Buchanan</a> to steal the 2000 U.S. presidential election, only to be stopped by the "X-Presidents" (a superhero group composed of <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_H.W._Bush" title="George H.W. Bush">George H.W. Bush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>).</li>
<li>Californian punk rock band <a href="/wiki/NOFX" title="NOFX">NOFX</a>'s 2003 song <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco_Un-American" title="Franco Un-American">Franco Un-American</a> includes the line "the President's laughing 'cause we voted for Nader", referring to Nader's possible role in inadvertently changing the result of the 2000 U.S. presidential election</li>
<li>The October 25, 2008 episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a></i> features <a href="/wiki/Bill_Hader" title="Bill Hader">Bill Hader</a> portraying Ralph Nader during a "<a href="/wiki/Weekend_Update" title="Weekend Update">Weekend Update</a>" sketch. In the sketch, <a href="/wiki/Seth_Meyers" title="Seth Meyers">Seth Meyers</a> asks Hader (as Nader) where he has been recently, to which Hader replies "food co-ops, armed compounds - I was at <a href="/wiki/Burning_Man" title="Burning Man">Burning Man</a> for a couple of days, that was kinda cool".</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.anunreasonableman.com/" rel="nofollow"><i>An Unreasonable Man</i></a> (2006). <i><a href="/wiki/An_Unreasonable_Man" title="An Unreasonable Man">An Unreasonable Man</a></i> is a documentary film about Ralph Nader that appeared at the 2006 Sundance Film Festival.</li>
<li>Burden, Barry C. (2005). <a class="external text" href="http://apr.sagepub.com/cgi/content/abstract/33/5/672" rel="nofollow">Ralph Nader's Campaign Strategy in the 2000 U.S. Presidential Election</a> 2005, <i>American Politics Research</i> 33:672-99.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.customflix.com/205780" rel="nofollow"><i>Ralph Nader: Up Close</i></a> This film blends archival footage and scenes of Nader and his staff at work in Washington with interviews with Nader's family, friends and adversaries, as well as Nader himself. Written, directed and produced by Mark Litwak and Tiiu Lukk, 1990, color, 72 mins. Narration by Studs Terkel. Broadcast on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Service" title="Public Broadcasting Service">PBS</a>. Winner, Sinking Creek Film Festival; Best of Festival, Baltimore Int'l Film Festival; Silver Plaque, Chicago Int'l Film Festival, Silver Apple, National Educational Film &amp; Video Festival.</li>
<li>Bear, Greg, "<a href="/wiki/Eon_(novel)" title="Eon (novel)">Eon</a>" –  the novel includes a depiction of a future group called the "Naderites" who follow Ralph Nader's humanistic teachings.</li>
<li>Martin, Justin. <i>Nader: Crusader, Spoiler, Icon.</i> Perseus Publishing, 2002. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/073820563X">ISBN 0-7382-0563-X</a>.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://library.cqpress.com/cqalmanac/document.php?id=cqal66-1301349" rel="nofollow">"Congress Acts on Traffic and Auto Safety"</a>. <i>CQ Almanac</i>. <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Quarterly" title="Congressional Quarterly">Congressional Quarterly</a>. 1966. pp. 266–268<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">April 27,</span> 2016</span>. <q>Breaking into the traffic safety inertia was the publication in November 1965 of “Unsafe At Any Speed,” a book written by Ralph Nader a 32-year-old Connecticut lawyer who had served as a consultant for the Department of Labor and a Senate subcommittee in 1964–65. House Speaker John W. McCormack (D Mass.) Oct. 21, 1966, credited the final outcome of the traffic safety bill to the “crusading spirit of one individual who believed he could do something: Ralph Nader”.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARalph+Nader&amp;rft.atitle=Congress+Acts+on+Traffic+and+Auto+Safety&amp;rft.date=1966&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Flibrary.cqpress.com%2Fcqalmanac%2Fdocument.php%3Fid%3Dcqal66-1301349&amp;rft.jtitle=CQ+Almanac&amp;rft.pages=266-268&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">Democratic<br/>
candidates</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><b>Nominee <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a></b></li>
<li>(<a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama_presidential_campaign,_2008" title="Barack Obama presidential campaign, 2008">campaign</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Barack_Obama" title="Political positions of Barack Obama">positions</a>)</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_vice_presidential_candidate_selection,_2008" title="Democratic Party vice presidential candidate selection, 2008">VP</a> nominee <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a></b></li>
<li>(<a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Joe_Biden" title="Political positions of Joe Biden">positions</a>)</li>
